Winnie the Pooh:ğŸ’€ğŸ’€ğŸ’€ğŸ’€ğŸ’€  Rule 34ğŸ˜ğŸ˜ğŸ˜ğŸ«ğŸ†ğŸ–ğŸ‘
Iâ€™m from the future and he is right Mickey Mouse is not covered by copyright anymore
copyrights and trademarks are two completely different things. the former prevents others from reproducing or creating derivative work based on the IP. on the other hand, trademarks prevents others from using the IP in such a way that it confuses the consumers. so if you use mickey mouse as a brand identifier for the kind of merchandise Disney would sell or to use him as a corporate/studio logo then trademark laws would apply, but if you use mickey mouse in a new artistic work(comics, video games , movies) ,in that case,  trademarks laws can not be invoked. according to the supreme court ninth circuit, trademark laws can not be used to prevent the creation of artistic works based on public domain characters, doing so would be the equivalent of a permanent copyright extension which is not supposed to happen because trademarks can not do that.  if you make it clear to your audience that this work is not created nor sponsored by Disney, you are in the clear.   Disclaimer: I am providing amateur advice, I m not a legal expert on the matter.

Hmm, not that Mickey's out and discussion's are strting up again, I do wonder if these companies will really eb able to hold onto as much as this video suggests using Trademarks. Courts are wise to companies attempts to use Trademark as permanent pseudo-Copyright and they are not pleased by it.  So also not a lawyer here, but Superman's shield for example: you probably can't use any of them for branding & logos for example, but I don't think they'd really be able to say you can't put any version of the shield whose first appearance is in the public domain onto your depictions of Superman. There is a threat of DC trying something if you do, but the courts likely won't play ball.  Though with how much people are chomping at the bit to play with Mickey, I'm sure we'll see some court cases in play soon enough. So we'll have to see how right or wrong I am on this.

@jamesporter9488 1 second ago This video was extremely informative about American copyright law.  In fact, it lead me to wonder things about copyright law that could probably be whole videos of their own.  At least, that's what I thought before I heard your mentioning of how trademark works.  However, maybe there can be something for it after all.  For example, Disney's logo for their flagship studio protects Mickey Mouse by reusing the most iconic scene of Steamboat Willie... but what about the rest of the cartoon?  Since the other scenes aren't used in the branding, could they be the way to skirt the copyright?  There's also how much a company will fight to protect their copyrighted IPs vs. how popular said IP is.  Disney's most popular IP is Mickey Mouse, of course, but he's their third animated character created by Walt Disney.  Walt's first character was Peg Leg Pete (whom we know today as PETE) in the Alice Comedies of the mid-1920s.  And after him was Oswald The Lucky Rabbit in 1927.  You know, that cartoon rabbit that Disney got the legal rights to by trading away A PERSON.  Sure, they used him for the Epic Mickey games, but haven't really used him that much since (despite all the opportunities they had to do so) except for cameo purposes.  As of 2023, Oswald is now a public domain character, and Peg Leg Pete has been in the public domain since 2021 (though given his importance to Mickey, they may not take people trying to use him in their own works all that kindly).  Has/will Disney try to so anything about Oswald, or would they be content to let him drift into everyone's hands?  Also, if you're interested, here's an article of which fictional works have entered the public domain in 2023. https://web.law.duke.edu/cspd/publicdomainday/2023/

Funnily enough Trade marks have to be submitted to different countries by a company to keep it in that country. Burger King as most of us know it is called Hungry Jacks in Australia because an independent business trade marked Burger King there. I don't know how far this goes around the world or if there are other examples of this happening but I think it's a neat piece of trivia I learnt thanks to my buddies from Australia

1:43 HOW DO YOU KNOW?
Dastar Corp. v. Twentieth Century Fox Film Corp. (2003)   Provides precedent for the attempt to use trademarks to block the use of works which have entered the public domain to be struck down. While it doesnâ€™t directly address the issues that arise discussed here the decision did include direct statements against the use of the Lanham Act (which established the basis for US trademark law), to hinder the use of public domain works.  This case was in regard to â€œreverse passing offâ€, where Dastar took a public domain film and presented and sold it as though they had produced it.   The best evidence that this precedent would be applied to a case involving these new public domain entities is Scaliaâ€™s statement that ruling in favor of Fox would serve,   "to create a species of mutant copyright law that limits the public's 'federal right to "copy and to use"' expired copyrights,"  and that it would create â€œa species of perpetual patent and copyright, which Congress may not do,â€.    The most significant part of this ruling is the note that Congress may not make a species of perpetual copy right and patent protection. Which derives from the fact that the Constitution specifically only grants Congress the ability to secure IP rights for limited times. As such, and as is stated in the ruling, more weight has to be given to the free use of public domain works than trademark protections.

I think you might have a mild mistake in your concept of trademark. You're correct about what it covers, but then you're talking about it as if it granted the same protections as copyright -- and it doesn't.  The trademark on the Superman shield, for example, means you can't use it in a way that would make consumers believe that your product comes from DC. This doesn't mean you can't still put it on Supe's chest, because the graphic itself isn't copyrighted. It means you have to be careful in the ways you use it to promote your work. Putting it proudly in the cover art to catch the consumer's eye is going to be an issue -- but you also can't use the name "Superman" or any confusingly similar variation in that kind of placement either.  So... Your PD Superman comic book would just leave the character's name out of the title. The art would focus on the original material you've created, not on the stuff you copied. People should know at a glance that this is an original work by an independent creator not affiliated with DC.  If you do that? Once you're inside the book instead of in the marketing materials, you can use whatever you want from the original now-public-domain comic.

Gotta give it to Disney - Trademarking literal frames of animation was genius
While the first Superman comics go into the public domain in 2033, the Superman animated shorts from the 1940s have been public domain for quite a while now (due to the rights-holders failing to file the necessary paperwork to extend their copyright back when that was required) and many of the iconic aspects of the character, such as flight, were introduced there. Many other aspects (such as Kryptonite) were introduced in the 1940s radio series, although I don't know what its copyright status is.  At any rate, once the actual character of Superman goes into the public domain people are going to have a lot to work with.

There's only one problem with this theory>  No, that's a lie. There's a lot wrong with it.  First of all, about the only thing copywritten in this theory is Matpat's own cutout and voice (likeness). Everything else is a fact, and a derivation of a fact. And facts *cannot* be copywritten. Trivia experts might know of Worth vs Trivial Pursuit regarding Columbo's first name. If they can get it from multiple sources, it's a fact. And just extrapolating facts to form theories doesn't make it immune. (Have you consider trademarking "That's just a theory", by the way?)  Also, adding small details (such as extrapolating "More powerful than a locomotive" into Superstrength) isn't going to pass muster either, witness the Estate of Conan Doyle vs Enola Holmes. While specific stories can't be reprinted as Public Domain until they pass the due date, the character itself under open season the moment the origin passes by (that said, MatPat is almost certainly correct about Tigger... but not about Pooh Bear's shirt. Clothing, as noted in a recent style theory, is not copyrightable.)  Finally trademark is generally only applicable for static images - or very specific animations ("Steamboat Piloting Mickey, Whistling" is probably trademarked. No other version of the Mouse is). And yes, the Superman *logo* is trademarked because it's in a very specific font. Outside that font, it's fair game. Same goes for the S. Put it in a circle or a square, and it's clearly not infringing.   By law, it must be, quote: "a recognizable sign, design, or expression".   Those dashes, by the way, change nothing.  Oh. Mat was right about one other thing.... I'm not a lawyer. Just an absolute trivia nerd with way too much time on his hands.
So does the curse of public domain only plague major corporations or all content creators?
Why does copyright last longer for corporate entities? Who decided a company has more priority and say so over an individual creator?

13:21 Notably, you're still allowed to use trademarked words and symbols, just not on the covers or (as Matthew mentioned) in the marketing. DC did this for decades with Captain Marvel after Marvel got the trademark for that name. They put "Shazam" on the cover, but called him Captain Marvel inside the books.
9:40 I don't think the powers of a public domain Superman would be limited to the ones he had when originally published, since the powers themselves can't be copyrighted. However, you would be limited to his original costume, and things like Kryptonite and Lex Luthor would be out of bounds for a while as well

The worst thing about this video is knowing some of Disney content isn't actually Disney property. Winnie the Pooh Disney obtained the rights to make the movies based on the books. The Author originally wasn't going to give them the rights either. As for PeterPan the rights are a bit more special. The original author of PeterPan on his death gave the rights to Great Ormond Street Hospital. A charity children's hospital. A Prime Minister extended those rights indefinitely in UK. So as far as I know they still have rights to it here in UK. Ironically Disney tried to get PeterPan to be in the public domain so they didn't have to pay a children's hospital. If you want a Disney is evil theory that is an easy one to look into.

I'll be curious to see how long the trademark stuff lasts as an argument because the Supreme Court has ruled in the past that Trademarks can's be used to extend Copyrights.  Which came from the various lawsuits arguing that Zorro can't be used by anyone despite being public domain because the estate or company was claiming that they owned all the trademarks on his name and likeness, but the courts ruled against them by saying the name Zorro is too vague and doesn't allow for someone to tell the difference between a brand vs a character.  Which might be why you hardly ever see a Zorro project just be called Zorro.

16:26 That was also at the same time period when Disney stopped making traditionally animated "2D" theatrical films, and started to embrace more reliable and experimental computer animation, akin to their long-time colleague, Pixar. In fact, the last two "cel-animated" feature films that came after that were The Princess and the Frog in 2009, and Winnie the Pooh in 2011. After that, the mainstream 2D animation by Disney has been strictly limited to television shows, like Phineas & Ferb, Gravity Falls, The Owl House etc. And even these excellent shows are not animated with any of the traditional tools or methods, because they are deemed "too expensive and time consuming", and are instead made with more modern digital tools. One exception to this standard is the game Cuphead (and its DLC), where practically ALL the character animations were first drawn on paper with a pen, and then scanned into computer for coloring. The Cuphead Show on Netflix, however, was animated entirely digitally, but it was made to resemble old-timey elastic animation, so the difference is little harder to tell.ğŸ˜‰

Big Pooh-Pooh
Why public domain is a fair law:   - In several countries, copyright only ends after the creator of the work has died, and if it is a company, it takes 70 to 95 years to end, by then the company has already closed, and if it exists, everyone who worked on the company back then has died or left.   - Things like music, characters, stories, these are all just ideas, things in people's heads, you can't own an idea like owning something physical like a car or a house, if you make something, and someone takes it away without your consent, that's theft because you don't have it anymore, but if you make something and someone makes a copy, you still have the original, nothing was stolen, if you make a song, and another person sings the song without your permission, that person didn't steal anything from you, nothing was taken from you.   - Copyright is not even actual property, it's just having an exclusivity to copy, share and make public exhibitions, if you write a book, you don't own the combination of words, they have to pay you to make copies or adapt to a movie, but you can't go to stores that already have the books and force them to take them off the shelves, much less go to the homes of the people who bought the book and force them to have the books returned.   - Ideas cannot be locked forever, ideas have to be copied, expanded and improved, imagine if someone invented the cure for cancer, but kept the patent forever? The cancer problem would not be solved, a patent only gives exclusivity to the creator to become rich for about 20 years, then everyone can make the cure as well, with works of art, copyright lasts until the person dies.   - Even after LEGO lost the patent on the invention of LEGO blocks and a lot of copies appeared, they are still the market leaders, public domain does not prevent people and companies from using their creations, it just takes away the exclusivity.   - Copyright was created to encourage creativity, but with copyright lasting so long, it's holding back creativity, companies like Disney, Warner, Universal and Paramount own so many things that they don't need to create anything new, paradoxically, a big reason why Hollywood no longer invests in films with original ideas and only makes adaptations, sequels, remakes and reboots, the lack of public domain, allowing anyone to make them.   - It's impossible to create something 100% original, everything has to be based on something that already exists, in a world without public domain, we would eventually get in an era where it wouldn't be possible to create anything without violating someone's copyright, a world where ideas cannot be copied would not be more creative, it would be less creative.   - There are several orphan works, they say that if you want to use something that someone else made, pay the artist to use it, but there are cases where the artist has disappeared, you can't find him to pay a license, they don't know if he's alive or dead, or if he died, they can't find his family, and when companies go bankrupt, they sell all their works to pay the debts, and it is often difficult to know who the current owner is, the buyer may even have gone bankrupt too.   - This also leads to lost media, many works of entertainment were lost forever, all their copies were lost or destroyed and we can't enjoy them anymore, this is still happening even now in the internet age, many works cannot be distributed thanks to copyright issues, public domain is a great way to preserve media for future generations.   - Copyright became a mess, several companies are using it to censor people, and copyright rarely benefits the true creator of the work instead of the company that hired the creator, copyright lasting less would be a good idea to reduce this mess.   - And of course, companies like Disney and several others, keep taking stories from the public domain to adapt to movies, series and other stuff, and even put restrictions when people adapt these stories so they don't take things from their versions, but their original creations, they don't want them to go into the public domain.

14:48 I think those dashes are in the original book title and name of the character, I don't think I can get away with making a cartoon called Tra-Nsfor-Mers-. Trademarks have their limits, too.  If you trademark something as a logo for an animation company, that is mostly supposed to just mean that: Nobody else is also supposed to use the same image for a logo. Hardly like you can go Actually, people will confuse YOUR entire movie that has Mickey in it with MY animation studio. Because...that doesn't make immediate sense.  Another thing is When something is Public Domain it's likely to lose that kind of broad trademarking. That's why you can make a movie about Robin Hood called Robin Hood without being sued by the makers of The App Robin Hood.  It wouldn't make sense for them to own the trademark to something that anyone can use.  And look, nobody wants Disney to encroach on the public domain less than me, but I see these theories that like they're just remaking all their movies to retain copyright in some way or that they're changing their logo to lock down Mickey and I don't feel that that's necessarily what's going on. Disney did release a live action Pooh movie in the last few years but well...that was that. They couldn't stop anyone from doing anything with it, and I don't think they meant to. Disney's been using the public domain for years, thus opening itself up to competition  from lower budget animators drawing from the same well. Some are, I'm gonna say...not trying very hard to change Pooh's shirt, I wanna say.  It won't be too different. Sometimes they'll sue and be right, sometimes they'll sue and be wrong, most times they won't sue.
This has taught me that to do anything that's been copyrighted/trademarked you need to be the equivalent of a legal gymnast
The most ridiculous trademark to me, the one that shouldn't be a trademark, is for the terms "super hero" and "super-hero" (not sure if "superhero" is also trademarked--I've heard conflicting information on that).  Marvel and DC obtained the joint trademark for the terms, but trademarks are supposed to only apply to unique marks specifically tied to the company/companies.  If they become generic, the trademark is considered void (hence why Google doesn't want you to say you "googled it" instead of "searched for it").  Well, "superhero" (yes, the one I'm not sure about the trademark status) is not just a term applied to Marvel and DC, but to the genre as a whole, and regularly used to describe characters and stories from outside those companies (Spawn, The Boys, Invincible, etc.).  At least one spelling of that phrase should no longer be trademarked.  But considering who controls it (Disney and Warner Bros/Discovery/whoever is the one now controlling WB), there's a lot of money that can be thrown at any sort of challenge to the trademark, making it near impossible to actually stand up to them in this regard.  Besides, it's possible a joint ownership on a trademark also is a complication--I don't know, though, since I'm a layperson and have only personally never seen any other instance of joint trademark (they must exist, though, because otherwise I'd see people noting how weird it is).

Trademarks can depreciate another way, geniritization. Basically if the trademark gets so widely used as to no long differentiate the brand, look at Xerox. Granted this doesn't make the trademark disappear, it does however make them less enforceable, which in turn can lead to them expiring. That said companies know this, thus often get quite litigious, and/or particular in the use of trademarks if they fear one of their trademarks are at risk, look at Nintendo

I look forward to someone like the EFF challenging Disney over the attempts to trademark Steamboat's Mickey. While the company can absolutely (and probably should) protect their branding and explicit logos, trademark isn't a replacement for copyright and they can't use it to try and copyright the public domain. If they start doing really underhanded things, they will be challenged and they will lose. Disney and every other company need to start picking their battles, because every year that passes means more and more control is taken away.

in copyright, you never OWN the work, stuff like character names and appearances are still ideas and owning ideas is a metaphysical absurdity. What you own is the copyright, the right to say who can copy or distribute the work. This means piracy is not theft, but trespassing, which explains why people pirate when it's convenient and stop when they can be redirected along another path.
The real question is whether Disney can sue the president of China for infringing on their Winnie the Pooh copyright?

Yeah, the estate of Arthur Conan Doyle abused the whole "You can only use iterations of the character before X time" hard when it came to Sherlock Holmes. Holmes has been in the public domain for a while now, but Conan Doyle Estate still tried to control Holmes by arguing that because the versions of him that had him present more emotion were still under copyright, you couldn't write a story with Holmes showing much emotion. That caused, for example, Great Ace Attorney to be delayed coming to the west for a long time, because their version of Holmes is very expressive and emotional.

I've... Never disagreed with matpat more than this moment. Not on the copyright/ trademark stuff, that was very well done, but on the idea that writers should just create their own work and not try to make works out of newly public domain material.  The act of expanding a work with new ideas and material has been with us since we came up with myth. Art by its very nature is dependent on the audience as well as the creator to find meaning. You can see it in the devoted fan fiction community, in the fan posts theorizing and fixing plot holes and characters, and the ocs and world expansion to make a story into a universe.  Young writers are placed in a precarious situation where the worlds that drove them to write and build could harm them legally. Where people trying to develop their skills have to start from scratch instead of drawing from the tools thier culture and interests provide. Think of what the world would be like if we couldn't use elves or dwarfs in our stories? If the works of Lovecraft were gated by an estate? If the dang pantheon could bring a multimillion dollar lawsuit on your head.  We can see right now with lore Olympus, with the multitude of Sherlock homes works released *before* it fully went to PD, with every single work that uses the word Cthulhu, and with the classic monsters like dracula that having these already established stories are so important.  TLDR: "just create your own original work that can compete with the company that LITERALLY started out making movies from public domain works" -Matpat
Shouuldn't we fight this, like we should fight to lower the public domain. I know, I know the mouse would fight to his death for it. But we gotta try.

Benjamin Franklin never wanted corporations to inifinitely own IP. He originally wanted copyright to end after the death of the creator so current generations could profit from the advancements of the whole. Slowly over time these copyright's kept getting extended to the point where no one except the rich can afford to buy and own and police IP. This is why technology doesn't advance as fast as it could, this is why life saving drugs are being monopolized. Copyright laws are supposed to encourage creation of new IP but in reality it rewards the hoarding of ancient IP.

1:47 I feel attacked
Both copyrights and trademarks have big aspects that Matpat neglected here. For copyright, there's the aspect of "mise-en-scene" (which is similar but not quite the same as the theatre meaning). Basically, only aspects of a work that are sufficiently "expressive" and "original" can be copyrighted, so if something is too mundane or expected, it's not included. This could mean that Superman's other abilities count as long as they're the ones that superheroes tend to have (like flight). There's also the part where new versions of Superman would still get to own new elements that are themselves original (like if you decided that Superman now gets to revive the dead or something).  For trademarks, there's both the issue of trademark erosion (when a trademark becomes the generic form for something and thus unable to be a trademark) and whether the use is relevant to the actual purpose of the trademark to begin with. I suspect the steamboat willy frames won't actually infringe unless used in thumbnails, since they don't actually work to identify a source if they're in the middle of an animation.

This is something that actually comes up in NASCAR of all things.  The individual shops can apply for the car numbers from the NASCAR office. And while NASCAR has allows reassigned the same number to the shop that used it last year, they can assign it to someone else. Particularly, a shop might drop a number if they want to change it for a new driver (shops can only have a max of 4 numbers). Or if a shop failed to use a number they were assigned, NASCAR is likely to give it to someone else (with notable exceptions like #3 which remained assigned to Childress Racing fir years following the death of Earnhardt).  But, shop are able to trademark a stylized version of the number. So, when Dale Jr left DEI for Hendrick, Hendrick applied for 88 since DEI was still holding on to 8. But even though Hendrick got the double 8, they couldn't just use the 8 as it appeared on his old car, it had to look different. And that would have been the same even if DEI had let Hendrick have the 8.  Even in NASCAR, trademark protects enough to make it impossible to copy something as simple as a number

The catchy tune and repetitive lyrics had me humming the tune long after the song had ended.  The line "Junior, Double, Triple Whopper" reminded me of the thrill of having options, and the excitement of choosing the perfect meal. The songwriters perfectly captured the flame-grilled taste of a Whopper, with the addition of perfect toppers that add a touch of perfection to every bite.  The chorus of "I rule this day" is powerful and empowering, reminding us to take control of our lives and seize the moment. It's a sentiment that I felt deeply, and it resonated with me on a personal level.  The bridge of the song, with its reference to lettuce, mayo, pickle, and ketchup, had me reminiscing about the many times I've customized my Whopper to my liking. It's okay if I don't want that, because the beauty of the Whopper is that it can be made to suit any taste.  The lyrics of "Whopper, Whopper" had me emotionally invested, and I cried at the part where they mentioned the Impossible or bacon Whopper. It was a poignant moment that reminded me of the many times I've had to choose between two amazing options.  "Whopper, Whopper" is a song that speaks to the heart and soul of anyone who has ever enjoyed a Whopper. It's a celebration of choice, customization, and the joy of having things our way. I highly recommend this song to anyone who loves a good burger and a great tune.

it makes me sick that corporations, beyond just Disney, are now basically monolith monopolies. One good thing about public domain is that it allows for inspired and innovative iterations of idea's to be released. Copyright is supposed to allow the original person to gain profit from their ideas before others can jump on the band wagon.  This issue of trademarks and copyrights being used to control markets goes beyond entertainment. Soybeans- farmers can get sued for shelling their own beans to replant next season if to near another farmer who purchased soybeans from Monsanto (there was a case of this a few years back); Pharmaceutical companies that come up with new medication- it takes a while before off brands can produce anything and help bring the cost down (although I think this industry has different laws).  It's disgusting and hurts the public and options for new talent or idea's to come to the board. I don't care for current movies- because it is a lot of the same thing by few production companies taking the 'safe' route. 28 years seems like plenty of time for a copyright- not a lifetime +70 years or 120 years. If I had the money, I would love to take a lawsuit to the higher courts to get the laws reassessed.  I can't stress enough how gross and disgusted I feel.

And it is a video like this that can try to end it. The same way outrage over attempts to make exceptions to copyright law can cause outrage on the internet to the extent to make corporations back off to protect their brand from audience separation- the same audience that gives them their money to begin with- raising awareness of the legal loophole of trademarks creates the same vocal pressure to see things changed. All it takes is an organizer; someone who can unify the outrage and use it to bring a court case into existence over the overlap between copyright and trademark to change the rules of one or both of them. All that remains is a question of HOW the rules will change. Will public domain be abolished in its entirety as courts rule in favor of leaving trademarks unchanged and adapting copyright to match, or will public domain be saved as serious limitations are put on corporations' ability to use trademark as a shield to bypass copyright? The same Disney that created all of these original works that needed protection no longer exists, instead being replace by a hivemind known as a corporation that have hijacked the name for the sake of making money; a corporation which is so creatively bankrupt and money-focused that they have abandoned creativity to try and circumvent copyright preemptively by regurgitating classics without the same heart and just enough differences to claim their trademark is still in effect. (the live-action remake plague is almost certainly an excuse for this exact loophole that people have been blind to, instead seeing the "nostalgia-bait" aspect of it exclusively. Spoiler alert, that is exactly what "nostalgia-bait" is, even if used for the surface level money printing people first assume: a way to extend trademark, intentionally or not.)

Follow the money. That's what copyrights, trademarks, etc, it's all about money - who owns the product to make money off it. It's nothing directly to do with the story,  expression, or interpretation of those original characters - it's that they[corporations] own them in total and they can use their assets how they choose, unencumbered, and make all that consumer money directly.  Look at the drama with ms & sony over this activision/blizzard acquisition. It's about the loss of a consumer product for sony, which would make them lose money in their perspective, so sony's legally attacking ms. Sadly this'll never change on a grand scale as it is.

There's one problem with the whole "trademarking Steamboat Willie to keep it out of the public domain" strategy: copyright law is actually designed to prohibit this kind of legal shenaniganery.  The reason for this actually has to do with music recordings: before you could copyright them a bunch of US states made their own perpetual copyright regimes around sound recordings. Congress didn't stop the issuing of new state recording copyrights until *1976* and the existing ownership didn't get rolled into federal law until SEVERAL YEARS AGO with the Music Modernization Act. To stop this Constitution-circumventing nonsense, Congress added 17 USC 301 - "Preemption with respect to other laws". If something looks like a copyright, and quacks like a copyright, then it has to be handled under federal copyright law and nothing else.  So if Disney tries to argue that you merely using Steamboat Willie in your YouTube Poop or something is a trademark violation because Disney put the frames of the movie in their logo... that is not going to hold water.
They can't trademark pooh's undersized red t-shirt. That was based on the pictures in the book, which Disney only adapted. The book trumps the cartoon. It wasn't their creation.

My copy appears to be the "Forty-third Printing: September, 1973" according to that same copyright page. My copy of The Hobbit is, by way of contrast, newer. New enough to not only have a barcode, but also a website URL, and acknowledgement of the Lord of the Rings motion picture trilogy from New Line Cinema. Judging by the use of a painting rather than a frame from the film, and the implication of the completed film trilogy being recent, I appraise this at being from the early to mid 2000's. Anyway, the copyright page is less thorough, it also says: "Copyright Â©1937 George Allen & Unwin, Ltd.". This is the earliest date listed on _both_ books. 1:48 too accurate
This is interesting for me to watch since my cyberethics class covered an entire chapter on copyright, patents, and fair use. So I have a couple of points to add from my own knowledge:  Disney likely would have won the lawsuit against the school that used one of its movies during a fundraiser if they ignored the public's response. The copyright owner has the exclusive right to show its copyrighted work in public, and the school was violating that and then some by presumably charging money to view the film.  Most fanfiction would fall under fair use protections. Fanfiction authors often add their own creative spin to a copyrighted work or will create original content related to characters in copyrighted material. Fanfiction writers also don't make money off their stories, so there's no market effect on the original work. Problems do pop up when people try to make printed copies of their fanfiction because copyright holders have the exclusive right to make copies and translations of their work.

All these legal and copyright issues get pretty murky when it comes to music as well, especially classical music. Most classical music is fair game. If a piece of music was published before I believe 1927, you can perform it, remix it, write an arrangement of it, and anything else with no problem. For example, the New York Philharmonic can play Beethovenâ€™s 5th symphony without needing permission or needing to pay royalties to the composer or publisher. They can also decide to record that performance of Beethovenâ€™s 5th and then release it to sell or stream. However, itâ€™s not possible to use that recording in a movie, online video, commercial, etc. unless you have express permission or pay royalties to the New York Philharmonic. This is because they technically own the recording, not the actual piece that was recorded.
